THE ABSOLUTE MUST

ChatGPT said:
Yep â€” RLS = Row Level Security.
This is the exact mechanism that turns everything you designed into something the backend physically cannot violate.

Iâ€™ll explain it plainly, then map it directly to your rules so it clicks.

What RLS actually is (plain English)
Row Level Security is a database rule that answers one question:

â€œIs this specific user allowed to see or change this specific row?â€

Not:

â€œAre they an admin?â€

â€œIs the UI hiding the button?â€

But:

â€œAt the data level, yes or no?â€

If the answer is â€œno,â€ the database refuses â€” even if:

the frontend is buggy

someone tampers with requests

an API route is misused

This is why RLS matters for your system.

Why RLS is critical for your design
Your system relies on negative guarantees:

admins can see but not touch

users canâ€™t affect other usersâ€™ organization

focus cannot be forced

ownership must transfer cleanly

team/org boundaries must never leak

Those guarantees cannot live in UI code alone.

RLS is how you enforce:

â€œThis is not allowed to exist.â€

How RLS fits your system (conceptual mapping)
Iâ€™ll map this to the major tables you implicitly designed.

1ï¸âƒ£ Org boundary (hard wall)
Rule

A user can only see rows belonging to their org.

RLS concept

row.org_id = auth.user.org_id
This applies to:

projects

milestones

items

focus

states

No cross-org leaks. Ever.

2ï¸âƒ£ Team visibility
Users

can only see items/projects for their team

Admins

can see all teams in the org

RLS logic

if user.role = admin â†’ allow

else â†’ row.team_id = user.team_id

Admins get visibility without mutation rights.

3ï¸âƒ£ Item ownership & editing
Rule

Only the current owner/assignee can edit an item

View access is broader

RLS split

SELECT: allowed broadly (team/org)

UPDATE / DELETE: allowed only if:

item.owner_id = auth.user.id

Once ownership transfers (user â†’ admin), the original user loses write access automatically.

No extra logic needed.

4ï¸âƒ£ Focus & queue control (critical)
Rule

Only the user can:

change their focus

reorder their waiting queue

RLS

row.user_id = auth.user.id
Admins:

can SELECT

cannot UPDATE or INSERT

So even if an admin wanted to force focus â€” the database says no.

5ï¸âƒ£ Assignment flow enforcement
Rule

Items must go through pending_acceptance

Only the recipient can accept or decline

RLS

INSERT into UserItemState allowed only if:

user_id = auth.user.id

UPDATE state allowed only if:

current user is the assignee

transition is valid

No silent assignment hacks.

6ï¸âƒ£ Needs-info loop protection
Rule

Declined items stay visible but inactive

Only creator can resubmit

Only recipient can accept

RLS

Creator: can UPDATE content

Recipient: can UPDATE state

Others: SELECT only

This enforces your back-and-forth loop structurally.

The important mental shift
RLS means:

Permissions are not features.
They are laws.

If later you:

add AI

add automation

add bulk actions

add integrations

Those things still cannot break your rules.

Because they hit the same database.

Why Supabase specifically works well here
Supabase:

runs RLS inside Postgres

ties auth.uid() directly to policies

makes read/write separation clean

supports â€œadmin can read, not writeâ€ naturally

Your system is actually ideal for Supabaseâ€™s model.

One sentence to lock in your head
If something must never happen, it belongs in RLS â€” not in code.

Youâ€™ve already designed the rules correctly.
RLS is just the enforcement layer.

BRIGHTLY
System Definition & Operating Model
(Conceptual â€“ Final)

STEP 1 â€” THE PROBLEM
Modern teams suffer from a systemic execution problem, not a tooling problem.

People are constantly interrupted by:

shifting priorities

informal reassignments

urgency disguised as importance

silent pressure from visibility

managers asking â€œwhat are you working on?â€ and â€œwhatâ€™s the progress?â€

This creates several failure modes:

Focus fragmentation

Anxiety-driven execution

Performative productivity

Engineers keeping work â€œactiveâ€ to look busy

PMs becoming human status middleware

Progress that exists only in peopleâ€™s heads

Existing tools optimize for tracking, not execution.
They overload users with priorities and allow silent interruption.

Brightly exists to solve focus, ownership, and visibility â€” not task tracking.

STEP 2 â€” THE USER
Primary users:

Individual contributors (engineers, admins, operators)

Project managers

Admins / managers / VPs

Key reality:

People can only meaningfully focus on one thing at a time.

People need agency over how they organize work.

Leadership needs visibility, not control.

Reprioritization is normal and healthy.

STEP 3 â€” WHAT WE ARE SOLVING
Brightly is solving three problems simultaneously:

Execution clarity

What should I work on right now?

Ownership clarity

Who owns this item?

Who can change it?

Progress clarity

How far are we?

What happened to this work?

Without asking people directly.

Brightly explicitly does NOT solve:

productivity scoring

effort measurement

velocity normalization

priority enforcement

STEP 4 â€” PROPOSED SOLUTION (HIGH LEVEL)
Brightly is:

a single-focus execution system

with a user-owned waiting queue

and full visibility without interference

combined with structural progress accounting

Execution (focus) and reporting (progress) are intentionally separated.

STEP 5 â€” MINIMUM VIABLE TRUTH (MVT)
These are non-negotiable truths the system must enforce.

Users own the order of what they focus on

New priorities are visible and must be consciously acknowledged

Priority creates awareness, not forced execution

Focus is singular and intentional

Reprioritization never erases work

Visibility must never imply control

If a feature violates these, it does not ship.

STEP 6 â€” BASE DOMAIN MODEL & EXTENSION RULES
6A â€” BASE DOMAIN MODEL (STRUCTURAL TRUTH)
This defines what exists, independent of behavior.

Org
Team
User
Project
Project Milestone
Item
Item Milestone (optional)

Rules:

Items may exist inside milestones or standalone.

Hierarchy describes context only, never execution.

Base entities do not encode behavior.

6B â€” BEHAVIORAL MODELS
Behavior lives outside base entities.

UserItemState:

user_id

item_id

state (pending_acceptance, needs_info, waiting, active, completed)

acknowledged_at

order_index

UserFocus:

user_id

item_id

started_at

ended_at

Rule:

A user can have exactly ONE active focus at any time.

6C â€” SCHEMA EVOLUTION RULES
Base entities never gain behavioral meaning

New features attach via new models

Existing meaning is never reinterpreted

Focus logic never lives on Item, Project, or Milestone

Hierarchy never implies execution

STEP 7 â€” DATA FLOW (STATE MACHINE)
Core Focus Rules
A user always has exactly ONE active item.

All other items are in waiting state.

Waiting is ordered and user-owned.

Focus Change
Switching focus moves the previous active item to the TOP of the waiting queue.

Completion
Completing the active item:

marks it completed

automatically activates the top waiting item

Item Creation
New items always go to the top of the waiting queue.

New items never replace the active item.

Assignment Rules
Admin â†’ User:

Item enters pending_acceptance

User must acknowledge and accept

Accept â†’ item goes to top of waiting queue

Decline â†’ item goes to needs_info with message

User â†’ Admin:

Same flow in reverse

Acceptance transfers ownership

Decline & Needs-Info Loop
Decline does NOT mean rejection.

Flow:

Item is assigned â†’ pending_acceptance

Recipient declines with message â†’ needs_info

Creator updates item

Creator resubmits â†’ pending_acceptance

Loop continues until accepted or canceled

Rules:

Items in needs_info are invisible to focus and waiting queues.

No item enters execution without acceptance.

No dangling or unanswered work exists.

STEP 8 â€” ADMIN VISIBILITY & REPORTING
Core principle:
Admins may see everything. Admins may not touch user organization.

Admins can see:

Active item per user

Full waiting queue (ordered)

Item details and history

Assignment states

Project / milestone / item progress

Admins cannot:

Change focus

Reorder queues

Activate items

Bypass acceptance

Inject work into execution

Visibility is unlimited. Control is zero.

STEP 9 â€” PROGRESS & COMPLEXITY MODEL
Progress Definition
Progress is derived from completed structure, not activity.

Item:

No milestones â†’ binary completion

With milestones â†’ completed milestones / total

Project Milestone:

completed items / total items

Project:

completed milestones / total milestones

Complexity Handling (Critical)
The system DOES NOT normalize complexity.

Instead:

Complex work can be structurally broken down using Item Milestones.

Simple work remains atomic.

Complexity is visible, not quantified.

The system does NOT measure:

effort

difficulty

time spent

velocity

busyness

Clarification:
The system refuses to make complex work comparable to simple work numerically.

STEP 10 â€” ENFORCEMENT & CONSTRAINTS
All philosophy must be enforceable at the data level.

Key invariants:

One active focus per user (hard constraint)

Acceptance gating before execution

Needs-info isolation

User-owned queue ordering

Deterministic auto-advance on completion

Progress is derived, never editable

Admins:

read-only for focus and organization

no mutation paths exist

If a rule matters, it must be enforced in data â€” not UI.

STEP 11 â€” UI CONTRACT & PERMISSIONS
User Permissions
Users can:

Create items, projects, project milestones, item milestones (for themselves)

Edit their own items IF they are the assignee

Delete their own items and structures

Organize their own queue

Control their own focus

Assign items to admins only

Ownership Rule:

When a user assigns an item to an admin and the admin accepts,
ownership transfers and the user loses edit control.

Users have read-only access to othersâ€™ items.

Admin Permissions
Admins can:

Do everything users can

Create projects

Set default assignees for project items

Assign items

Respond to needs-info

Cancel items they own

Admins cannot:

Control focus

Reorder queues

Edit user organization

Visibility Boundaries
Team:

Users see only their teamâ€™s data

Admins see all teams

Org:

No cross-org visibility

Here is STEP 1 rewritten completely in paragraph form, slow, detailed, and faithful to everything we talked about. No bullets. No lists. Just clear, human explanation.

Step 1 â€” The Problem
This step exists to explain why Brightly needs to exist in the first place. Before talking about features, structure, or behavior, it is important to clearly describe the real problem Brightly is trying to solve. If this problem is misunderstood or watered down, everything that follows becomes vague or unnecessary.

The core problem is not that people are bad at managing tasks. The real problem is that modern work constantly pulls people in multiple directions at the same time. People are expected to make progress on many things at once, respond quickly to new requests, and still show visible progress, even though they can only truly focus on one thing at a time.

In most teams today, work systems allow many items to appear â€œactiveâ€ at the same time. New requests can be added instantly, priorities can shift silently, and nothing prevents work from piling up in a way that overwhelms the person doing it. This creates an environment where people are technically assigned to many things, but are realistically making progress on only one.

Because of this mismatch, progress becomes unclear. Managers and leaders cannot reliably tell what someone is actually working on or how far along something really is. The system itself does not answer these questions clearly, so people are forced to ask each other directly. This turns project managers and team leads into human status-checkers, constantly asking questions like â€œWhat are you working on right now?â€ or â€œWhy did this stop?â€ or â€œHow far along is this?â€

For individuals, this creates constant pressure. People feel anxious about choosing what to work on next because everything feels important. They may keep tasks marked as â€œin progressâ€ just to avoid questions, even if they are not actively working on them. They switch context frequently, not because it is effective, but because it is expected. Over time, this leads to stress, shallow focus, and the feeling of being busy without feeling productive.

For managers and leaders, the lack of clear progress visibility leads to repeated check-ins, more meetings, and decisions based on partial information. Instead of trusting the system, they rely on conversations, which are slow, inconsistent, and easy to misinterpret. This often increases pressure on individuals, even when the intention is simply to understand what is happening.

Teams suffer as a result. Trust in tools decreases because the system does not reflect reality. Work appears stalled even when it is moving forward, or appears active even when it is blocked. Reprioritization feels chaotic rather than intentional. Over time, people stop believing that the system is telling the truth.

Many existing tools try to solve this problem by adding more features: more priority levels, more statuses, more deadlines, more reports. However, this usually makes things worse. Instead of becoming clearer, the system becomes louder. More signals compete for attention, and visibility turns into pressure rather than understanding. These tools often mix execution and reporting in a way that encourages people to perform activity instead of make real progress.

This step accepts several uncomfortable truths that most systems avoid. A person can only meaningfully focus on one thing at a time. Reprioritization is normal and does not mean failure. Progress cannot be honestly measured by effort or busyness. Visibility without structure creates pressure, not clarity. Ignoring these truths leads to systems that look organized but feel chaotic to the people using them.

Brightly does not assume people need more discipline or motivation. It does not blame individuals for being overwhelmed. It treats this as a system design problem, not a personal failure. The goal is not to make people work harder or faster, but to create an environment where focus is protected, ownership is clear, and progress can be understood without constant interruption.

The conclusion of this step is simple: the problem Brightly is solving is not task tracking. It is the lack of a system that respects human focus while still giving leaders honest visibility into progress. Brightly exists to reduce silent pressure, eliminate the need for constant explanations, and allow people to work calmly on one thing at a time while still making progress visible to everyone who needs to see it.

When youâ€™re ready, say â€œDo Step 2â€ and weâ€™ll continue at the same depth and tone.

Step 4 â€” The Proposed Solution
Brightly proposes a simple but intentional way of working that matches how people actually focus and how managers actually need to see progress. Instead of trying to manage everything at once, the system separates doing the work from seeing the work, so neither gets in the way of the other.

At the center of the solution is the idea that a person should only be actively working on one thing at a time. Everything else they need to do still exists, but it waits in an ordered list that the user controls. This removes the pressure of juggling multiple â€œactiveâ€ tasks while still keeping future work visible and organized.

For managers and admins, the solution does not involve pushing priorities or interrupting execution. Instead, they are given clear visibility into what is being worked on, what is waiting, and how work is progressing over time. This visibility replaces the need for constant check-ins, status meetings, or follow-up messages.

What the solution looks like in practice
Each user always has one clearly defined active item

All other work lives in a waiting list that the user organizes

New work never interrupts what someone is currently doing

Managers can see progress without changing user priorities

Progress is shown based on completed work, not activity

Reprioritization is allowed without losing history

What this solution avoids on purpose
Forcing priorities onto users

Measuring effort or busyness

Allowing silent interruptions

Letting visibility turn into control

This solution works because it accepts human limits instead of fighting them. By protecting focus, preserving user control, and making progress visible without interference, Brightly creates a calmer, more honest way for teams to move work forward together.

Step 5 â€” The Rules That Must Not Break

These rules exist to keep Brightly calm, fair, and truthful. They ensure that focus is protected, ownership is respected, and progress can be understood without stress or guesswork.

Step 7 â€” How Work Moves Day to Day
This step explains how work actually flows through Brightly once it exists in the system. It focuses on everyday behavior: what happens when someone starts work, switches focus, finishes something, or receives new work. The goal is to remove surprises and make execution feel steady and predictable.

How focus and waiting work
Each user always has one clearly active item

Everything else waits in a list owned by the user

Switching focus sends the previous item back to the top of the waiting list

Finishing an active item automatically activates the next waiting item

New items always go into the waiting list

New items never interrupt what someone is currently working on

How work is handed off
Work assigned to someone must be accepted before it counts

If the work is unclear, it can be declined with an explanation

Declined work stays visible until it is clarified

Once clarified, the work can be offered again

This loop continues until both sides agree the work is ready

This flow removes silent interruptions and unclear handoffs. People always know what they are working on, what is waiting, and why something has not moved yet. Work moves forward intentionally, not reactively.

Step 10 â€” Making the Rules Impossible to Ignore

# Brightly: Focus
## System Plan

---

## Purpose of This Document

This document captures the complete system thinking behind **Brightly: Focus**.

It exists to:
- preserve intent
- prevent design drift
- avoid re-deriving decisions
- act as a single source of truth

This is **not** a feature list.
This is **not** a technical spec.
This is a system plan.

If future work contradicts this document, the work is wrong â€” not the document.

---

## Design Sequence (Locked)

The system was designed following this order:

1. System Plan (this document)
2. System Architecture
3. Domain Model
4. Data Schema
5. Build

Skipping or reordering these steps introduces risk.

---

## Step 1 â€” The Problem

Modern work systems overload people with priorities while failing to show real progress.

People can only focus on one thing at a time, yet systems allow many things to appear active.
This creates interruption, anxiety, and unclear ownership.

Managers cannot see progress clearly, so they ask.
PMs become human status trackers.
Progress lives in conversations instead of systems.

Brightly exists to protect focus while making progress visible without interruption.

---

## Step 2 â€” The Users

Brightly is built for:
- people doing focused work
- managers who need visibility
- teams with shared responsibility

The system assumes:
- focus is singular
- reprioritization is normal
- users need agency
- managers need clarity, not control

---

## Step 3 â€” What Is Being Solved

Brightly solves:
- execution clarity (what to work on now)
- ownership clarity (who owns what)
- progress clarity (how far along without asking)

Brightly does NOT solve:
- productivity scoring
- effort tracking
- performance comparison
- priority enforcement

---

## Step 4 â€” The Proposed Solution

Brightly separates execution from visibility.

Users work on one active item at a time.
All other work waits in a user-controlled order.
Managers can see progress without interfering.

The system stays calm by design.

---

## Step 5 â€” Rules That Must Not Break

- One active item per user
- Users own their execution order
- New work requires acceptance
- Reprioritization never erases progress
- Visibility never grants control
- Progress reflects completion, not activity

If these break, the system fails.

---

## Step 6 â€” System Building Blocks

Brightly understands:
- Organization
- Team
- User
- Project
- Project Milestone
- Item
- Optional Item Milestones

Structure provides context, not execution rules.

---

## Step 7 â€” How Work Moves

Users always have one active item.
Everything else waits.

Switching focus moves the previous item to the top of waiting.
Completing work auto-advances the next item.

Assignments require acceptance.
Declines trigger a clarification loop.
Nothing interrupts silently.

---

## Step 8 â€” Visibility Without Control

Managers can see:
- active work
- waiting work
- history
- progress

Managers cannot:
- change focus
- reorder work
- force execution

Visibility answers questions.
Control creates pressure.
Brightly allows the first and forbids the second.

---

## Step 9 â€” Honest Progress

Progress comes from finishing work, not effort.

Small work is done or not done.
Large work can be broken into steps.

The system does not measure:
- effort
- time
- complexity
- busyness

Complexity is shown through structure, not numbers.

---

## Step 10 â€” Enforcement

Rules are enforced by the system, not memory or discipline.

- Single focus is guaranteed
- Acceptance is required
- Waiting work cannot interrupt
- Progress cannot be edited

If a rule matters, it must be enforced.

---

## Step 11 â€” Permissions

Users:
- control their own work
- organize their own queue
- edit work while they own it
- view others read-only

Admins:
- see everything
- assign work with acceptance
- define structure

Admins cannot control execution.

---

## Step 12 â€” Completion Criteria

The system is complete when:
- users feel focused
- managers stop asking for updates
- progress is self-explanatory
- reprioritization feels safe
- the system feels quieter than alternatives

No extra intelligence is required.

---

## Final Position

Brightly is:
- a focus engine
- a progress ledger

Brightly refuses to:
- score productivity
- normalize complexity
- enforce priorities
- enable silent interruption

This document is the systemâ€™s memory.
